<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Red" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Flammability code 0: Will not burn. E.g., water">0</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:15px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Blue" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Health code 2: Intense or continued but not chronic exposure could cause temporary incapacitation or possible residual injury. E.g., chloroform">2</span></a></div><div style="width:13px; text-align:center; position:absolute; top:31px; left:54px;">
<a href="/wiki/NFPA_704#Yellow" title="NFPA 704"><span style="color:black;" title="Reactivity code 0: Normally stable, even under fire exposure conditions, and is not reactive with water. E.g., liquid nitrogen">0</span></a></div></div></div></div>
</td></tr>




</tbody></table>
<p>Mercury and most of its compounds are extremely toxic and must be handled with care; in cases of spills involving mercury (such as from certain <a href="/wiki/Mercury-in-glass_thermometer" title="Mercury-in-glass thermometer">thermometers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fluorescent_light_bulb" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluorescent light bulb">fluorescent light bulbs</a>), specific cleaning procedures are used to avoid exposure and contain the spill.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Protocols call for physically merging smaller droplets on hard surfaces, combining them into a single larger pool for easier removal with an <a href="/wiki/Eyedropper" class="mw-redirect" title="Eyedropper">eyedropper</a>, or for gently pushing the spill into a disposable container. Vacuum cleaners and brooms cause greater dispersal of the mercury and should not be used. Afterwards, fine <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, or some other powder that readily forms an amalgam (alloy) with mercury at ordinary temperatures is sprinkled over the area before itself being collected and properly disposed of. Cleaning porous surfaces and clothing is not effective at removing all traces of mercury and it is therefore advised to discard these kinds of items should they be exposed to a mercury spill.
</p><p>Mercury can be absorbed through the skin and mucous membranes and mercury vapors can be inhaled, so containers of mercury are securely sealed to avoid spills and evaporation. Heating of mercury, or of compounds of mercury that may decompose when heated, should be carried out with adequate ventilation in order to minimize exposure to mercury vapor. The most toxic forms of mercury are its <a href="/wiki/Organic_compounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic compounds">organic compounds</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Dimethylmercury" title="Dimethylmercury">dimethylmercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methylmercury" title="Methylmercury">methylmercury</a>.  Mercury can cause both chronic and acute poisoning.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Releases_in_the_environment">Releases in the environment</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mercury_in_Ice_Core_Upper_Fremont_Glacier.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Mercury_in_Ice_Core_Upper_Fremont_Glacier.svg/220px-Mercury_in_Ice_Core_Upper_Fremont_Glacier.svg.png" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Mercury_in_Ice_Core_Upper_Fremont_Glacier.svg/330px-Mercury_in_Ice_Core_Upper_Fremont_Glacier.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Mercury_in_Ice_Core_Upper_Fremont_Glacier.svg/440px-Mercury_in_Ice_Core_Upper_Fremont_Glacier.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="744" data-file-height="1052" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mercury_in_Ice_Core_Upper_Fremont_Glacier.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amount of atmospheric mercury deposited at Wyoming's Upper Fremont Glacier over the last 270 years</div></div></div>
<p>Preindustrial deposition rates of mercury from the atmosphere may be about 4&#160;ng /(1 L of ice deposit). Although that can be considered a natural level of exposure, regional or global sources have significant effects. Volcanic eruptions can increase the atmospheric source by 4–6 times.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Natural sources, such as <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>, are responsible for approximately half of atmospheric mercury emissions. The human-generated half can be divided into the following estimated percentages:<sup id="cite_ref-Pacyna_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pacyna-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>65% from stationary combustion, of which <a href="/wiki/Coal-fired_power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Coal-fired power plant">coal-fired power plants</a> are the largest aggregate source (40% of U.S. mercury emissions in 1999). This includes power plants fueled with gas where the mercury has not been removed. Emissions from coal combustion are between one and two orders of magnitude higher than emissions from oil combustion, depending on the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Pacyna_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pacyna-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>11% from gold production. The three largest point sources for mercury emissions in the U.S. are the three largest gold mines. Hydrogeochemical release of mercury from gold-mine tailings has been accounted as a significant source of atmospheric mercury in eastern Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>6.8% from <a href="/wiki/Non-ferrous_metal" title="Non-ferrous metal">non-ferrous metal</a> production, typically <a href="/wiki/Smelter" class="mw-redirect" title="Smelter">smelters</a>.</li>
<li>6.4% from <a href="/wiki/Cement" title="Cement">cement</a> production.</li>
<li>3.0% from <a href="/wiki/Waste_disposal" class="mw-redirect" title="Waste disposal">waste disposal</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Municipal_waste" class="mw-redirect" title="Municipal waste">municipal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hazardous_waste" title="Hazardous waste">hazardous waste</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crematoria" class="mw-redirect" title="Crematoria">crematoria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sewage_sludge" title="Sewage sludge">sewage sludge</a> incineration.</li>
<li>3.0% from <a href="/wiki/Caustic_soda" class="mw-redirect" title="Caustic soda">caustic soda</a> production.</li>
<li>1.4% from <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> production.</li>
<li>1.1% from mercury production, mainly for batteries.</li>
<li>2.0% from other sources.</li></ul>
<p>The above percentages are estimates of the global human-caused mercury emissions in 2000, excluding biomass burning, an important source in some regions.<sup id="cite_ref-Pacyna_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pacyna-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent atmospheric mercury contamination in outdoor urban air was measured at 0.01–0.02&#160;µg/m<sup>3</sup>. A 2001 study measured mercury levels in 12 indoor sites chosen to represent a cross-section of building types, locations and ages in the New York area. This study found mercury concentrations significantly elevated over outdoor concentrations, at a range of 0.0065&#160;– 0.523&#160;μg/m<sup>3</sup>. The average was 0.069&#160;μg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mercury also enters into the environment through the improper disposal (e.g., land filling, incineration) of certain products. Products containing mercury include: auto parts, <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battery (electricity)">batteries</a>, fluorescent bulbs, medical products, thermometers, and thermostats.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Due to health concerns (see below), <a href="/wiki/Waste_management" title="Waste management">toxics use reduction</a> efforts are cutting back or eliminating mercury in such products. For example, the amount of mercury sold in thermostats in the United States decreased from 14.5 tons in 2004 to 3.9 tons in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most thermometers now use pigmented <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a> instead of mercury, and <a href="/wiki/Galinstan" title="Galinstan">galinstan</a> alloy thermometers are also an option. Mercury thermometers are still occasionally used in the medical field because they are more accurate than alcohol thermometers, though both are commonly being replaced by electronic thermometers and less commonly by galinstan thermometers. Mercury thermometers are still widely used for certain scientific applications because of their greater accuracy and working range.
</p><p>Historically, one of the largest releases was from the Colex plant, a lithium-isotope separation plant at Oak Ridge, Tennessee. The plant operated in the 1950s and 1960s. Records are incomplete and unclear, but government commissions have estimated that some two million pounds of mercury are unaccounted for.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A serious <a href="/wiki/Industrial_disasters" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial disasters">industrial disaster</a> was the dumping of mercury compounds into <a href="/wiki/Minamata" class="mw-redirect" title="Minamata">Minamata</a> Bay, Japan. It is estimated that over 3,000 people suffered various deformities, severe mercury poisoning symptoms or death from what became known as <a href="/wiki/Minamata_disease" title="Minamata disease">Minamata disease</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Tobacco plant">tobacco plant</a> readily absorbs and accumulates <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a> such as mercury from the surrounding soil into its leaves. These are subsequently inhaled during <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">tobacco smoking</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> While mercury is a constituent of <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoke" title="Tobacco smoke">tobacco smoke</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-TalhoutSchulz2011_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TalhoutSchulz2011-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> studies have largely failed to discover a significant correlation between smoking and Hg uptake by humans compared to sources such as occupational exposure, fish consumption, and <a href="/wiki/Amalgam_tooth_fillings" class="mw-redirect" title="Amalgam tooth fillings">amalgam tooth fillings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sediment_contamination">Sediment contamination</span></h3>
<p>Sediments within large urban-industrial <a href="/wiki/Estuaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Estuaries">estuaries</a> act as an important sink for point source and diffuse mercury pollution within catchments.<sup id="cite_ref-VaneBeriro2015_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VaneBeriro2015-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> A 2015 study of foreshore sediments from the <a href="/wiki/Thames_estuary" class="mw-redirect" title="Thames estuary">Thames estuary</a> measured total mercury at 0.01 to 12.07&#160;mg/kg with mean of 2.10&#160;mg/kg and median of 0.85&#160;mg/kg (n=351).<sup id="cite_ref-VaneBeriro2015_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VaneBeriro2015-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The highest mercury concentrations were shown to occur in and around the city of <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> in association with fine grain muds and high total organic carbon content.<sup id="cite_ref-VaneBeriro2015_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VaneBeriro2015-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The strong affinity of mercury for carbon rich sediments has also been observed in salt marsh sediments of the <a href="/wiki/River_Mersey" title="River Mersey">River Mersey</a> mean of 2&#160;mg/kg up to 5&#160;mg/kg.<sup id="cite_ref-VaneJones2009_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VaneJones2009-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> These concentrations are far higher than those shown in salt marsh river creek sediments of New Jersey and mangroves of Southern China which exhibit low mercury concentrations of about 0.2&#160;mg/kg.<sup id="cite_ref-VaneHarrison2008_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VaneHarrison2008-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VaneHarrison2009_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VaneHarrison2009-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occupational_exposure">Occupational exposure</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EPA_workers_clean_up_residential_mercury_spill_(3986684199).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/EPA_workers_clean_up_residential_mercury_spill_%283986684199%29.jpg/220px-EPA_workers_clean_up_residential_mercury_spill_%283986684199%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/EPA_workers_clean_up_residential_mercury_spill_%283986684199%29.jpg/330px-EPA_workers_clean_up_residential_mercury_spill_%283986684199%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/EPA_workers_clean_up_residential_mercury_spill_%283986684199%29.jpg/440px-EPA_workers_clean_up_residential_mercury_spill_%283986684199%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EPA_workers_clean_up_residential_mercury_spill_(3986684199).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>EPA workers clean up residential mercury spill in 2004</div></div></div>
<p>Due to the health effects of mercury exposure, industrial and commercial uses are regulated in many countries. The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">OSHA</a>, and <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">NIOSH</a> all treat mercury as an occupational hazard, and have established specific occupational exposure limits. Environmental releases and disposal of mercury are regulated in the U.S. primarily by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_and_symptoms_of_mercury_poisoning">Effects and symptoms of mercury poisoning</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mercury_poisoning" title="Mercury poisoning">Mercury poisoning</a></div>
<p>Toxic effects include damage to the brain, kidneys and lungs. Mercury poisoning can result in several diseases, including <a href="/wiki/Acrodynia" title="Acrodynia">acrodynia</a> (pink disease), Hunter-Russell syndrome, and <a href="/wiki/Minamata_disease" title="Minamata disease">Minamata disease</a>.
</p><p>Symptoms typically include sensory impairment (vision, hearing, speech), disturbed sensation and a lack of coordination. The type and degree of symptoms exhibited depend upon the individual toxin, the dose, and the method and duration of exposure. <a href="/wiki/Case%E2%80%93control_studies" class="mw-redirect" title="Case–control studies">Case–control studies</a> have shown effects such as tremors, impaired <a href="/wiki/Cognitive" class="mw-redirect" title="Cognitive">cognitive</a> skills, and sleep disturbance in workers with chronic exposure to mercury vapor even at low concentrations in the range 0.7–42&#160;μg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-ngim_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ngim-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-liang_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-liang-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> A study has shown that acute exposure (4–8 hours) to calculated elemental mercury levels of 1.1 to 44&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup> resulted in chest pain, <a href="/wiki/Dyspnea" class="mw-redirect" title="Dyspnea">dyspnea</a>, cough, <a href="/wiki/Hemoptysis" title="Hemoptysis">hemoptysis</a>, impairment of pulmonary function, and evidence of interstitial <a href="/wiki/Pneumonitis" title="Pneumonitis">pneumonitis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Acute exposure to mercury vapor has been shown to result in profound central nervous system effects, including psychotic reactions characterized by delirium, hallucinations, and suicidal tendency. Occupational exposure has resulted in broad-ranging functional disturbance, including <a href="/wiki/Erethism" title="Erethism">erethism</a>, irritability, excitability, excessive shyness, and insomnia. With continuing exposure, a fine tremor develops and may escalate to violent muscular spasms. Tremor initially involves the hands and later spreads to the eyelids, lips, and tongue. Long-term, low-level exposure has been associated with more subtle symptoms of erethism, including fatigue, irritability, loss of memory, vivid dreams and depression.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span></h4>
<p>Research on the treatment of mercury poisoning is limited. Currently available drugs for acute mercurial poisoning include chelators N-acetyl-D, L-<a href="/wiki/Penicillamine" title="Penicillamine">penicillamine</a> (NAP), <a href="/wiki/British_Anti-Lewisite" class="mw-redirect" title="British Anti-Lewisite">British Anti-Lewisite</a> (BAL), <a href="/wiki/2,3-dimercapto-1-propanesulfonic_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="2,3-dimercapto-1-propanesulfonic acid">2,3-dimercapto-1-propanesulfonic acid</a> (DMPS), and <a href="/wiki/Dimercaptosuccinic_acid" title="Dimercaptosuccinic acid">dimercaptosuccinic acid</a> (DMSA). In one small study including 11 construction workers exposed to elemental mercury, patients were treated with DMSA and NAP.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chelation_therapy" title="Chelation therapy">Chelation therapy</a> with both drugs resulted in the mobilization of a small fraction of the total estimated body mercury. DMSA was able to increase the excretion of mercury to a greater extent than NAP.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish">Fish</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mercury_in_fish" title="Mercury in fish">Mercury in fish</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">Fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a> have a natural tendency to concentrate mercury in their bodies, often in the form of <a href="/wiki/Methylmercury" title="Methylmercury">methylmercury</a>, a highly toxic organic compound of mercury. Species of fish that are high on the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">shark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swordfish" title="Swordfish">swordfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_mackerel" title="King mackerel">king mackerel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bluefin_tuna" title="Bluefin tuna">bluefin tuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albacore_tuna" class="mw-redirect" title="Albacore tuna">albacore tuna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tilefish" title="Tilefish">tilefish</a> contain higher concentrations of mercury than others. As mercury and methylmercury are fat soluble, they primarily accumulate in the <a href="/wiki/Viscera" class="mw-redirect" title="Viscera">viscera</a>, although they are also found throughout the muscle tissue.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> When this fish is consumed by a predator, the mercury level is accumulated. Since fish are less efficient at depurating than accumulating methylmercury, fish-tissue concentrations increase over time. Thus species that are high on the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a> amass body burdens of mercury that can be ten times higher than the species they consume. This process is called <a href="/wiki/Biomagnification" title="Biomagnification">biomagnification</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mercury_poisoning" title="Mercury poisoning">Mercury poisoning</a> happened this way in <a href="/wiki/Minamata,_Kumamoto" title="Minamata, Kumamoto">Minamata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, now called <a href="/wiki/Minamata_disease" title="Minamata disease">Minamata disease</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulations">Regulations</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International">International</span></h3>
<p>140 countries agreed in the <a href="/wiki/Minamata_Convention_on_Mercury" title="Minamata Convention on Mercury">Minamata Convention on Mercury</a> by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> (UNEP) to prevent emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The convention was signed on 10 October 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h3>
<p>In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a> is charged with regulating and managing mercury contamination. Several laws give the EPA this authority, including the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(United_States)" title="Clean Air Act (United States)">Clean Air Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Water_Act" title="Clean Water Act">Clean Water Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Resource_Conservation_and_Recovery_Act" title="Resource Conservation and Recovery Act">Resource Conservation and Recovery Act</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Safe_Drinking_Water_Act" title="Safe Drinking Water Act">Safe Drinking Water Act</a>. Additionally, the <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Containing_and_Rechargeable_Battery_Management_Act" title="Mercury-Containing and Rechargeable Battery Management Act">Mercury-Containing and Rechargeable Battery Management Act</a>, passed in 1996, phases out the use of mercury in batteries, and provides for the efficient and cost-effective disposal of many types of used batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-epa_regs_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epa_regs-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> North America contributed approximately 11% of the total global anthropogenic mercury emissions in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The United States <a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(1990)" class="mw-redirect" title="Clean Air Act (1990)">Clean Air Act</a>, passed in 1990, put mercury on a list of toxic pollutants that need to be controlled to the greatest possible extent. Thus, industries that release high concentrations of mercury into the environment agreed to install maximum achievable control technologies (MACT). In March 2005, the EPA promulgated a regulation<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> that added power plants to the list of sources that should be controlled and instituted a national <a href="/wiki/Emissions_trading" title="Emissions trading">cap and trade</a> system. States were given until November 2006 to impose stricter controls, but after a legal challenge from several states, the regulations were struck down by a federal appeals court on 8 February 2008. The rule was deemed not sufficient to protect the health of persons living near coal-fired power plants, given the negative effects documented in the EPA Study Report to Congress of 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-nj_vs_epa_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nj_vs_epa-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> However newer data published in 2015 showed that after introduction of the stricter controls mercury declined sharply, indicating that the Clean Air Act had its intended impact.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The EPA announced new rules for coal-fired power plants on 22 December 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>  Cement kilns that burn hazardous waste are held to a looser standard than are standard <a href="/wiki/Hazardous_waste" title="Hazardous waste">hazardous waste</a> <a href="/wiki/Incinerator" class="mw-redirect" title="Incinerator">incinerators</a> in the United States, and as a result are a disproportionate source of mercury pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, the directive on the Restriction of the Use of Certain Hazardous Substances in Electrical and Electronic Equipment (see <a href="/wiki/Restriction_of_Hazardous_Substances_Directive" title="Restriction of Hazardous Substances Directive">RoHS</a>) bans mercury from certain electrical and electronic products, and limits the amount of mercury in other products to less than 1000 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">ppm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-eu_regs_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eu_regs-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> There are restrictions for mercury concentration in packaging (the limit is 100 ppm for sum of mercury, <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hexavalent_chromium" title="Hexavalent chromium">hexavalent chromium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a>) and batteries (the limit is 5 ppm).<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> In July 2007, the European Union also banned mercury in non-electrical measuring devices, such as thermometers and barometers. The ban applies to new devices only, and contains exemptions for the health care sector and a two-year grace period for manufacturers of barometers.
<sup id="cite_ref-eu_reuters_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eu_reuters-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Norway">Norway</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> enacted a total ban on the use of mercury in the manufacturing and import/export of mercury products, effective 1 January 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-norway_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norway-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> In 2002, several lakes in Norway were found to have a poor state of mercury pollution, with an excess of 1&#160;µg/g of mercury in their sediment.<sup id="cite_ref-environment_norway_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-environment_norway-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
In 2008, Norway’s Minister of Environment Development Erik Solheim said: "Mercury is among the most dangerous environmental toxins. Satisfactory alternatives to Hg in products are
available, and it is therefore fitting to induce a ban."<sup id="cite_ref-fda.gov_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fda.gov-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweden">Sweden</span></h3>
<p>Products containing mercury were banned in Sweden in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Denmark">Denmark</span></h3>
<p>In 2008, Denmark also banned dental mercury amalgam,<sup id="cite_ref-fda.gov_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fda.gov-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> except for molar masticating surface fillings in permanent (adult) teeth.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Senese1997-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Senese1997_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Senese1997_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Senese, F. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://antoine.frostburg.edu/chem/senese/101/periodic/faq/why-is-mercury-liquid.shtml">"Why is mercury a liquid at STP?"</a>. General Chemistry Online at Frostburg State University. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070404210838/http://antoine.frostburg.edu/chem/senese/101/periodic/faq/why-is-mercury-liquid.shtml">Archived</a> from the original on 4 April 2007<span class="reference-accessdate">.
